We see some interesting patterns: For numbers like 6, f(6)=6; 18, f(18)=18; 42, f(42)=42; 54, f(54)=54; 66, f(66)=66; 78, f(78)=78; 30? f(30)=31 not fixed. But some numbers are fixed points: 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, etc. These are numbers where the sum of three largest proper divisors equals the number itself. That suggests that if a_n is such a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant: a_{n+1}=a_n. So the sequence is defined for all n (since it remains in the fixed point). So any a_1 that is a fixed point works. Are there other fixed points? Let's characterize numbers N such that f(N)=N.

From our earlier observation, f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4. For N to be fixed, we need N = N/d2+N/d3+N/d4 => 1 = 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4. So d2,d3,d4 are divisors >1 such that 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4=1. Also note that d2 is the smallest prime factor (since it's the smallest divisor >1, it must be prime). d2 is the smallest prime factor p. Then we need three divisors d2,d3,d4 (increasing) such that sum of reciprocals is 1. And d2,d3,d4 are divisors of N. Also note that N must be composite and not a prime square? But it could be a prime power or have distinct primes.

We need to find all positive integers N (with at least three proper divisors) satisfying that condition.

Let p = d2, the smallest prime factor. Then 1/p + 1/d3 + 1/d4 = 1. Since d3,d4 are divisors of N, they are at least p (since p is smallest). Also d3 <= d4.

We can solve for possible triples (p, d3, d4) of integers >1 such that 1/p+1/d3+1/d4=1. Since p is smallest, p <= d3 <= d4.

We can enumerate possibilities.

We have 1/d3+1/d4 = 1 - 1/p = (p-1)/p.

Since d3,d4 >= p, we have 1/d3+1/d4 <= 2/p. So (p-1)/p <= 2/p => p-1 <=2 => p<=3. So p=2 or p=3.

Case p=2: then 1/d3+1/d4 = 1 - 1/2 = 1/2. So we need two divisors d3,d4 >=2 such that 1/d3+1/d4=1/2. Since d3 <= d4, we have 1/d3 >= 1/d4, so 2/d3 >= 1/2 => d3 <=4. Also d3 >=2. So far, many numbers fail. But some numbers like 72 work, and it's not a fixed point itself. So we need to characterize all a_1 for which the sequence is defined for all n.

We need to find all a_1 such that the recursion a_{n+1}=f(a_n) always yields a term with at least three proper divisors, and the sequence is infinite. Since the recursion is deterministic, we need to find all starting numbers that eventually land on a fixed point (or a cycle that consists of numbers with at least three proper divisors) and then stay there. But note that if the sequence reaches a number that is prime or prime square, it fails because the next term is not defined (or the term itself fails the condition). So the sequence must never hit such numbers. So we need to find all a_1 such that the orbit under f stays within the set S of integers with at least three proper divisors (i.e., composite and not a prime square). Moreover, the sequence must be infinite, so it must not terminate. Since f is defined for all numbers in S, the sequence will be infinite as long as it stays in S. So we need to determine which a_1 in S have orbits that never leave S.

We can think of f as a function on positive integers >1, but it's only defined on S. However, if we apply f to a number not in S, the definition is not given, but we can still compute f(N) if N has at least three proper divisors? Actually the problem says each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so the recursion is only defined for such numbers. So we only consider a_1 that are in S, and then we compute a_2 = f(a_1). If a_2 is not in S, then the condition that a_2 has at least three proper divisors fails, so the sequence cannot be defined (since the next term would require three proper divisors of a_2, which doesn't exist). So a_1 must be such that the entire forward orbit stays in S.

Thus we need to characterize all numbers N in S such that iterating f never yields a prime or prime square.

We already have fixed points (numbers where f(N)=N). Those are clearly in S and their orbit is constant, so they work.

We also have numbers that map to a fixed point in one step, like 72 -> 78. So 72 works.

Are there numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? Possibly. For example, a_1= something that maps to 72? Let's see if any number maps to 72. We need f(N)=72. Solve N/d2+N/d3+N/d4=72, with d2,d3,d4 smallest divisors >1. This might have solutions. Then if N is in S and its orbit eventually reaches fixed point, then N works. But also there might be cycles of length >1 that consist entirely of numbers in S. Let's investigate cycles.

We need to study f more thoroughly.

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We have characterization: f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r, where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1, in increasing order. But careful: This formula holds when N has at least three proper divisors. But if N has exactly three proper divisors? For N=8, p=2,q=4,r=8? Actually r=8 is N itself, but N is not a proper divisor, but we used N/d2,N/d3,N/d4. d4=N, so N/d4=1. That's fine. So formula holds with d2,d3,d4 being the three smallest divisors >1, where d4 can be N. So it's consistent.

Now, note that d2 is always a prime (smallest prime factor). d3 is either the square of that prime or another prime factor. d4 is either the cube of the prime or product of two primes, etc. So f(N) is often less than N? Not always. For fixed points, f(N)=N. For other numbers, we can have f(N) > N or < N. Let's compute f(N) for some N: N=6, f=6; N=8, f=7>6? Actually 7>6? 8->7, f(8)=7<8? 7<8. Thus it appears that the only preimages of fixed points are numbers that map to them in one step, and the only fixed points are numbers M with smallest prime factor 2, second smallest divisor 3, third smallest divisor 6, i.e., M=6k with k odd and 5∤k. Thus it seems that the only numbers that map to fixed points are the fixed points themselves and numbers that map to them in one step, like 72->78. Are there other numbers that map to 78? Let's think about the function f more abstractly. For any N, define the sum of the three largest proper divisors. There is a known concept: The sum of the three largest proper divisors of N is equal to N * (1/p+1/q+1/r). This is a multiplicative function in the sense that it depends on the smallest three divisors. For numbers that are not prime powers, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r, where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors. So f(N) = N * (1/p+1/q+1/r). Since p,q,r are at least 2, the sum is at most 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12, so f(N) > N if the triple is (2,3,4) (since 13/12>1), and f(N) = N if the triple is (2,3,6) (since 1/2+1/3+1/6=1), and f(N) < N if the triple is (2,4,5) (since 1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1). So the behavior depends on the relative sizes.

Actually if N has at least three proper divisors, then total divisors at least 4? Actually divisors: 1, plus at least three proper divisors, plus N = at least 5? Wait: For N=8, divisors:1,2,4,8. Proper divisors:1,2,4 (three). So total divisors 4. So d2=2,d3=4,d4? There is no d4 because only divisors:1,2,4,8. So d2=2,d3=4,d4=8? But 8 is N, not proper. So the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,4,8. Then N/d2=4 (largest proper), N/d3=2 (second largest proper), N/d4=1 (third largest proper). So that gives the three largest proper: 4,2,1. That works. So indeed we can always take d2,d3,d4 as the three smallest divisors >1, where d4 can be N if N has exactly three proper divisors? Actually for N=8, we have d2=2,d3=4,d4=8 (N itself). Then N/d4=1 is proper. So it's fine. For N=12, d2=2,d3=3,d4=4 (all proper). So the three largest proper divisors are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4. So f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4, where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1 (in increasing order). That's a nice characterization.

Now we want to find all a_1 such that the sequence never hits a number that is prime or prime square? Actually it must have at least three proper divisors at each step, so we need that for all n, a_n is not prime and not a square of a prime. The recursion: a_{n+1}=f(a_n). So we need to study the dynamics of f.

We suspect that the only possible values of a_1 are numbers that are either 8? Or maybe 6? Let's test small numbers.

But some numbers are fixed points: 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, etc. These are numbers where the sum of three largest proper divisors equals the number itself. That suggests that if a_n is such a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant: a_{n+1}=a_n. So the sequence is defined for all n (since it remains in the fixed point). So any a_1 that is a fixed point works. Are there other fixed points? Let's characterize numbers N such that f(N)=N.

From our earlier observation, f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4. For N to be fixed, we need N = N/d2+N/d3+N/d4 => 1 = 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4. So d2,d3,d4 are divisors >1 such that 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4=1. Also note that d2 is the smallest prime factor (since it's the smallest divisor >1, it must be prime). d2 is the smallest prime factor p. Then we need three divisors d2,d3,d4 (increasing) such that sum of reciprocals is 1. And d2,d3,d4 are divisors of N. Also note that N must be composite and not a prime square? But it could be a prime power or have distinct primes.

We need to find all positive integers N (with at least three proper divisors) satisfying that condition.

Let p = d2, the smallest prime factor. Then 1/p + 1/d3 + 1/d4 = 1. Since d3,d4 are divisors of N, they are at least p (since p is smallest). Also d3 <= d4.

We can solve for possible triples (p, d3, d4) of integers >1 such that 1/p+1/d3+1/d4=1. Since p is smallest, p <= d3 <= d4.

We can enumerate possibilities.

We have 1/d3+1/d4 = 1 - 1/p = (p-1)/p.

Since d3,d4 >= p, we have 1/d3+1/d4 <= 2/p. So (p-1)/p <= 2/p => p-1 <=2 => p<=3. So p=2 or p=3.

Case p=2: then 1/d3+1/d4 = 1 - 1/2 = 1/2. So we need two divisors d3,d4 >=2 such that 1/d3+1/d4=1/2. Since d3 <= d4, we have 1/d3 >= 1/d4, so 2/d3 >= 1/2 => d3 <=4. Also d3 >=2. We need to find all a_1 such that the recursion a_{n+1}=f(a_n) always yields a term with at least three proper divisors, and the sequence is infinite. Since the recursion is deterministic, we need to find all starting numbers that eventually land on a fixed point (or a cycle that consists of numbers with at least three proper divisors) and then stay there. But note that if the sequence reaches a number that is prime or prime square, it fails because the next term is not defined (or the term itself fails the condition). So the sequence must never hit such numbers. So we need to find all a_1 such that the orbit under f stays within the set S of integers with at least three proper divisors (i.e., composite and not a prime square). Moreover, the sequence must be infinite, so it must not terminate. Since f is defined for all numbers in S, the sequence will be infinite as long as it stays in S. So we need to determine which a_1 in S have orbits that never leave S.

We can think of f as a function on positive integers >1, but it's only defined on S. However, if we apply f to a number not in S, the definition is not given, but we can still compute f(N) if N has at least three proper divisors? Actually the problem says each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so the recursion is only defined for such numbers. So we only consider a_1 that are in S, and then we compute a_2 = f(a_1). If a_2 is not in S, then the condition that a_2 has at least three proper divisors fails, so the sequence cannot be defined (since the next term would require three proper divisors of a_2, which doesn't exist). So a_1 must be such that the entire forward orbit stays in S.

Thus we need to characterize all numbers N in S such that iterating f never yields a prime or prime square.

Thus no preimage of 72 with p=2 and q=5.

What about p=2, q=6? Then q=6, so N divisible by 2,3,6. The third smallest divisor could be 6? But q=6, then the second smallest is 3? Actually if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then the second smallest divisor is 3, not 6. So q cannot be 6 because then the second smallest divisor would be 3, not 6. The triple would be (2,3,6) if N not divisible by 4 or 5. But if N is divisible by 2,3,6, and also maybe by 4? Actually if N is divisible by 6, it's divisible by 2 and 3. The smallest prime factor is 2, then the next divisor could be 3 if 3 divides N, which it does. So the second smallest divisor is 3, not 6. So q=6 is not possible as the second smallest divisor because 3 is always a divisor if 6 divides N? Not necessarily: if N is divisible by 6, it's divisible by 2 and 3. So 3 is a divisor. So the second smallest divisor is at most 3. So q can be 3 if 3 divides N and no divisor between 2 and 3, which is always the case. So q=3 is the only possibility for the second smallest divisor when p=2 and 3 divides N. So we cannot have q=6. So the only possible q for p=2 is either 3 (if 3 divides N) or 4 (if 3 does not divide N). So we have covered those.

Now consider p=3. Then N is odd. The three smallest divisors >1 are: p=3, then q could be 3^2=9 if 9 divides N, or another prime r>3 if 3 does not divide N? Actually if p=3, the second smallest divisor could be 9 if 9 divides N, or could be another prime r if N is divisible by a prime r>3 and 9 does not divide N. But also there could be prime factor 3? Actually p=3 is the smallest prime factor, so N is odd and divisible by 3. So 3 is a divisor. The second smallest divisor could be either 9 (if 9 divides N) or another prime factor q (if q < 9? Since 3 is smallest, q could be 5 or 7? But 5 and 7 are less than 9, so they could be divisors. So the second smallest divisor could be 5 if 5 divides N and 3 is smallest, then the order is 3,5,? So q=5. Similarly, q=7. Also q=9 if 9 divides N and no prime factor between 3 and 9 (i.e., 5,7). So we need to consider these subcases.

Case p=3, q=5. Then triple (3,5,r) with r>5. Then S = 1/3+1/5+1/r = 8/15+1/r. Then N = 72/(8/15+1/r) = 72/((8r+15)/(15r)) = 1080r/(8r+15). We already tested r=7,9,11,... and found no integer? So 72 is not a preimage of 72; it's a preimage of 78. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That is, f(N) = M where M = 6k, k odd, 5∤k. Write f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r = M. So N = M pqr/(pq+pr+qr). Since N must be an integer, and also p,q,r must be the three smallest divisors of N. This is a kind of Diophantine condition. We can try to solve for possible p,q,r.

Since M is fixed point, M = 6k with k odd, 5∤k. Also M is even, so p must be 2 (because if p>2, then N is odd, but M is even, so f(N) = sum of three divisors of N, all odd? If N is odd, its divisors are odd, so sum of three odd numbers is odd. So M must be odd. But M is even. So N cannot be odd. Therefore p=2. So the smallest prime factor is 2. So N is even. Then we have two subcases: q=3 (if 3 divides N) or q=4 (if 3 does not divide N). Because the second smallest divisor could be 3 if 3 divides N, or 4 if 3 does not divide N (since 4 is the next divisor after 2 if N is not divisible by 3, and also possibly if N is divisible by 4 but not by 3, then the second smallest divisor is 4). Actually if N is even and not divisible by 3, the smallest divisor >1 is 2, the next divisor could be 4 if 4 divides N, or could be another prime like 5 if N is divisible by 5 and not by 3 or 4? But if N is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor. But we need the second smallest divisor. If N is even and not divisible by 3, the possible divisors: 2,4,5,6,... The second smallest divisor is the smallest divisor >2. That could be 4 if 4 divides N, or could be 5 if 5 divides N and 4 does not, etc. So we need to consider general q, not just 3 or 4. However, the triple (p,q,r) must be such that N = M pqr/(pq+pr+qr) is an integer and also that the three smallest divisors of N are exactly p,q,r. This is a restrictive condition.

We can try to analyze the equation N = M pqr/(pq+pr+qr). Since M is fixed point, M is even. Also p=2. So N = M * 2qr/(2q+2r+qr) = 2Mqr/(qr+2q+2r). So N integer implies (qr+2q+2r) divides 2Mqr. Also we need that the second smallest divisor of N is q, and the third is r. Since N is even, q and r are divisors of N. Also, there should be no divisor d such that 2<d<q, and no divisor d such that q<d<r.

Now, note that M itself is a fixed point, so M = 6k with k odd, 5∤k. Then M is divisible by 6. So M is divisible by 2 and 3. So 3 divides M. Since N = 2Mqr/(qr+2q+2r), we have that N is divisible by 2 and M, so N is divisible by 2 and 3? Possibly.

We can try to find all possible q,r that satisfy the condition. Let's denote S = qr+2q+2r. Then N = 2Mqr/S. Since M is divisible by 6, we can write M = 6K, with K odd and 5∤K. Then N = 12Kqr/S. So N integer implies S | 12Kqr.

Also, we need that the three smallest divisors of N are 2, q, r. Since N is even, the smallest divisor >1 is 2. Then q is the second smallest. So we need that N has no divisor d with 2<d<q. In particular, q must be the smallest divisor greater than 2. So q must be prime? Actually if q is composite, it could be that a divisor of N less than q exists, but if q is composite, it might have a proper divisor d that is also a divisor of N. For example, if q=6, then divisors of N could include 3 and 2? Actually if N is divisible by 6, then 3 is a divisor, and 3<6, so the second smallest divisor would be 3, not 6. So q cannot be composite unless it is a prime power? But even then, if q is composite, say q=9, then if N is divisible by 9, it is divisible by 3, so 3 is a divisor, making second smallest divisor 3, not 9. So q must be prime. Because the second smallest divisor after 2 must be the smallest divisor >2. So if q is composite, its proper divisors include a divisor d < q, and if d divides N, then d is a divisor of N, contradicting that q is the second smallest. However, it's possible that d does not divide N? But if N is divisible by q, and q is composite, then any divisor of q is a divisor of N? Not necessarily: if N = q * t, and q is composite, then a divisor d of q is also a divisor of N? Yes, because if d|q, then q = d * s, so N = d * s * t, so d divides N. So any divisor of q is a divisor of N. Therefore, if q is composite, then its proper divisors are less than q and divide N, so they would be smaller than q, contradicting that q is the second smallest divisor. Therefore q must be prime. So q is an odd prime (since q>2). So q is an odd prime.

Now, r is the third smallest divisor. So r > q, and N has no divisor d such that q<d<r. Also, r must be a divisor of N. And r could be composite or prime. But if r is composite, its proper divisors are less than r and divide N? If r is composite, then any divisor d of r divides N because if r|N, then N = r * s, and if d|r, then r = d * u, so N = d * u * s, so d divides N. So if r is composite, then its proper divisors are less than r and divide N. For these to be not less than q? Actually if r is composite, it has a divisor d such that d < r. Could d be less than q? If so, then there is a divisor d < q, contradicting that q is the second smallest. If d is between q and r, then that would be a divisor d with q<d<r, contradicting that r is the third smallest. Therefore r must be such that all its proper divisors are either < q (but then they would be less than q and divide N, contradicting that q is the second smallest) or > q? Actually if r has a proper divisor d, then d < r. If d < q, then d is a divisor of N that is less than q, so the second smallest divisor would be at most d, not q. So that cannot happen. If d is between q and r, then that's a divisor between q and r, contradicting that r is the third smallest. Therefore r must have no proper divisors at all, i.e., r must be prime. Because if r is composite, it has a proper divisor d. Since d < r, we need to consider its relation to q. If d < q, then d is a divisor of N less than q, so q is not the second smallest. If d > q, then d is a divisor of N with q<d<r, so r is not the third smallest. So the only way to avoid contradiction is that r is prime. Therefore both q and r are primes, with q < r.

Given the complexity, maybe we can look for a known solution pattern. I recall a similar problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. Given the recursive nature, perhaps we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then f(N) is always greater than N? Or less? We saw that for numbers like 12, f(12)=13>12, but 13 is prime, so fails. Let's think about the function f more abstractly. For any N, define the sum of the three largest proper divisors. There is a known concept: The sum of the three largest proper divisors of N is equal to N * (1/p+1/q+1/r). This is a multiplicative function in the sense that it depends on the smallest three divisors. For numbers that are not prime powers, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r, where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors. So f(N) = N * (1/p+1/q+1/r). Since p,q,r are at least 2, the sum is at most 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12, so f(N) > N if the triple is (2,3,4) (since 13/12>1), and f(N) = N if the triple is (2,3,6) (since 1/2+1/3+1/6=1), and f(N) < N if the triple is (2,4,5) (since 1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1). So the behavior depends on the relative sizes.

Now, the fixed points are exactly those numbers where the triple is (2,3,6). So they are numbers that are divisible by 2 and 3, and have no divisor between 3 and 6, i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5. So they are numbers of the form 2*3*m with m odd and not divisible by 5. So the fixed points are those numbers.

Maybe we should consider the orbit of numbers that are multiples of 12. The map on N is: N -> 13N/12. So the sequence of numbers in the orbit is: N0, N1=13N0/12, N2=13N1/12 = 169N0/144, etc. So after k steps, we get Nk = (13^k / 12^k) N0. For this to eventually become a fixed point, we need that at some step, the factor 13^k/12^k becomes such that the number is of the form 2*3*m. This is equivalent to the condition that N0 is of the form 12^k * 2*3*m / 13^k = 2*3*12^k * m / 13^k. Since N0 must be integer, 13^k must divide 2*3*12^k*m. Since 13 and 12 are coprime, 13^k must divide m. So we can write m = 13^k * m'. Then N0 = 2*3*12^k * m' = 72 * 12^{k-1} * m'. So N0 is a multiple of 72. But also, the process of repeatedly applying f to a number that is a multiple of 12 is not just multiplication by 13/12; it's only valid if the number is divisible by 12 at each step. That is, if N0 is divisible by 12, then N1 = 13N0/12 is integer. But for N1 to be divisible by 12, we need that N0 is divisible by 12^2? Actually N1 = 13N0/12. For N1 to be divisible by 12, we need N0 to be divisible by 12^2? Because N1/12 = 13N0/144. So N1 divisible by 12 iff 144 divides 13N0. Since 13 and 144 are coprime, this is equivalent to 144 divides N0. So if N0 is divisible by 12^2 = 144, then N1 is divisible by 12. So the process continues: at each step, we need the current number to be divisible by 12 to apply the same transformation. So the numbers that work are those where the orbit eventually reaches a fixed point. The orbit can be described by the exponents of 2 and 3 in the number. For a number N that is a multiple of 12, write N = 2^a * 3 * u, with a>=2, u odd. Then f(N) = 13 * 2^{a-2} * u. So the new number has exponent of 2: a-2. So to continue, we need a-2 >= 2, i.e., a>=4. If a=3, then after one step we get a number with exponent 1, which is a fixed point if the number is of the form 2*3*m. That number is 26*u = 2*13*u. For it to be a fixed point, we need u divisible by 3 and u/3 odd and not multiple of 5. So that gives the condition for numbers with a=3. If a=4, then after one step we get a number with exponent 2, which is not divisible by 4? Actually exponent 2 means the number is divisible by 4. Then we need to compute f of that number. If the number is of the form 2^2 * 13 * u, and it is divisible by 12? Not necessarily. So the orbit may not stay in the form divisible by 12. But we can analyze the exponent of 2.

Let’s define the function g on the exponent of 2 and the factor 13 and 3. For numbers that are multiples of 12, we have: N = 2^a * 3 * u, with a>=2, u odd. Then f(N) = 13 * 2^{a-2} * u. So the exponent of 2 in f(N) is a-2. So if a is odd, then after k steps we get exponent a-2k, which will eventually become 1 when a-2k=1, i.e., k=(a-1)/2. At that step, we get a number of the form 2^1 * 13^k * u' (where u' is some odd number). That number is N_{k} = 2 * 13^k * u'. For it to be a fixed point, we need N_{k} to be of the form 2*3*m with m odd and 5∤m. <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
We need to find all positive integers a_1 such that the infinite sequence a_n defined by a_{n+1}=S(a_n) (where S(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N) exists (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors) for all n.

We have the following characterizations:
- Proper divisors of N are divisors other than N itself. The three largest proper divisors of N are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1 (if N is not a prime power, and if N is a prime power, the three largest proper divisors are the three largest powers of the prime less than N). So S(N)=N(1/p+1/q+1/r).

- Fixed points (S(N)=N) occur exactly when {p,q,r} = {2,3,6}, i.e., when N is divisible by 6, not by 4, and not by 5. So fixed points are numbers of the form 2·3·k with k odd and 5∤k.

- If N is divisible by 12, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4|N and 3|N). So S(N)=13N/12. This reduces the exponent of 2 by 2.

- If N is divisible by 6 but not by 4, then it is a fixed point if also not divisible by 5. Otherwise, it may be a fixed point or may increase.

We tested many numbers and found that the only numbers that seem to work (i.e., the orbit stays in S for all n) are:
- Fixed points: numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5. (i.e., N=6k with k odd and 5∤k)
- Numbers that map to a fixed point in one step: these are numbers N such that S(N) is a fixed point. This condition is equivalent to N being a multiple of 72 and N/72 being odd and not divisible by 5. (i.e., N=72·u with u odd and 5∤u)
- The number 864 (which is 72·12) works, but 12 is even and divisible by 5? No, 12 is even and not divisible by 5. So it's not of that form. However, 864 maps to 936 (which is 72·13) and then to fixed point 1014. So 864 is a preimage of a number that maps to a fixed point.

Thus, the set of valid a_1 is the union of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point under iteration of S. We claim that the possible values of a_1 are all positive integers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5. 
Proof: Let S(N) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N. 
We first show that if a_1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, then the sequence a_n defined by a_{n+1}=S(a_n) exists for all n and each a_n has at least three proper divisors. 
We prove by induction on n that a_n is always a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5. 
For n=1, it is true by assumption. 
Assume a_n is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5. 
We consider two cases: 
Case 1: a_n is not divisible by 4. Then a_n is a fixed point, i.e., S(a_n)=a_n. Since a_n is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, it is a fixed point (this is known). Thus a_{n+1}=a_n, which is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, so the property holds. 
Case 2: a_n is divisible by 4. Then a_n is divisible by 12. The three smallest divisors of a_n are 2,3,4. Hence S(a_n)=13a_n/12. This number is an integer and is also a multiple of 6?
